  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_c7f69556-a774-4df4-9d2f-e3c4a2518568'
      });

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer2232388921498954752">This captivating and exuberant tale is told by Gus Orviston, an irreverent young fly fisherman and one of the most appealing heroes in contemporary American fiction. <p>Leaving behind a madcap, fishing-obsessed family, Gus decides to strike out on his own, taking refuge in a secluded cabin on a remote riverbank to pursue his own fly-fishing passion with unrelenting zeal. But </p></span>
  <span id="freeText2232388921498954752" style="display:none">This captivating and exuberant tale is told by Gus Orviston, an irreverent young fly fisherman and one of the most appealing heroes in contemporary American fiction. <p>Leaving behind a madcap, fishing-obsessed family, Gus decides to strike out on his own, taking refuge in a secluded cabin on a remote riverbank to pursue his own fly-fishing passion with unrelenting zeal. But instead of finding fishing bliss, Gus becomes increasingly troubled by the degradation of the natural world around him and by the spiritual barrenness of his own life. His desolation drives him on a reluctant quest for self-discovery and meaning, ultimately fruitful beyond his wildest dreams. </p><p>Here, then, is a funny, sensitive, unforgettable story about the relationships among men, women, the environment, and the human soul.</p></span>
  <a data-text-id="2232388921498954752" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>

<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/4902455-carol-anne"><img alt="" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/f_100x100-6433b08cbe793211f55e184d39e84c4d.png" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/4902455-carol-anne">Carol Anne</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">I believe that a child that age would need to be a very precocious reader? I had a test with my own pr (precocious reader) who would come to me with m<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">I believe that a child that age would need to be a very precocious reader? I had a test with my own pr (precocious reader) who would come to me with my copies of books, asking to read it. The Hobbit, in third grade, for example, I had him read three pages and give me a synopsis, so I could check his grasp of the vocabulary, and his grasp of the overall gist of the book. If he could pass those "tests" I would agree to the reading. <br /><br />My own son was 16 when he read TRW, it totally captivated his heart and soul, he is 44 now, and it the book that binds us completely. If you have read it yourself (I am assuming so) then you know what the stumbling blocks would be, and you can make an educated guess on the effects that might concern you. I adore that you have the good fortune of a reader on your hands! <a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>
</div>

<div id="review_600269774" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/600269774" />
    <a title="Dolors" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18383778-dolors"><img alt="Dolors" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1502350013p2/18383778.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/600269774?book_show_action=true">Apr 26, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dolors" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dolors" href="/user/show/18383778-dolors">Dolors</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">The river flows in you</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Dolors by: </span>
      The voices in my head
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18383778-dolors?shelf=dost">dost</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18383778-dolors?shelf=read-in-2016">read-in-2016</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer600269774" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16012585115319577279">There comes a time when the growing frustration with the generally unfair paradoxes of existence becomes so unbearable that one needs to gain distance from himself to see clearly, to listen with the eyes and hear with the heart.<br/>Or simply one reaches a moment when action and emotional implication start to feel so forced, so disjointed, that a clean break is needed to reconnect again with the invisible chord of communion that binds us to others.<br/><br/>Like the convoluted, meandering river that exists onl</span>
  <span id="freeText16012585115319577279" style="display:none">There comes a time when the growing frustration with the generally unfair paradoxes of existence becomes so unbearable that one needs to gain distance from himself to see clearly, to listen with the eyes and hear with the heart.<br />Or simply one reaches a moment when action and emotional implication start to feel so forced, so disjointed, that a clean break is needed to reconnect again with the invisible chord of communion that binds us to others.<br /><br />Like the convoluted, meandering river that exists only in the flowing, as the Tao prophezied, life is bestowed upon us with all its unfathomable mysteries to be pondered about until the gnawing void of indifference dampens the impatient eagerness of childhood days.<br />That is what happens to Gus Orviston, who at the age of twenty is tired of struggling against the numbness that has utterly obliterated his zest for life. Born into an eccentric family that is obsessed with fly-fishing, Gus embarks on a lone trip to regain his inner balance. <br />Seeking solitude at the riverbanks of a remote rural area in Oregon, he settles down in a small wooden cabin that is surrounded by a dense grove of cedars that shrouds the location with their drooping branches.<br />Secluded and isolated in and out, the disenchanted young man believes he will fill the hollowness that threatens to engulf him by devoting all his mental strength to perfecting his already off-the-charts fishing techniques.<br />Weeks turn into months and by the time Gus realizes he is adrift in the vastness of his nothingness, he recoils from the sting of that so much desired individual freedom that now seems useless in the face of such desolate loneliness. He craves for human touch and that is the tipping point in his pilgrimage towards self-discovery.<br /><br />Duncan’s novel is much more complex and vibrant than the archetypal existential bildungsroman because he explores the limits of spirituality beyond the frames of institutionalized religion while drawing a symmetric allegory between Gus’ restless meditations on his relation with the world, his place in it and the art of fly-fishing. The result is an incredibly well-rounded metaphor, an uncommonly hybrid book composed of metaphysical pondering, self-effacing humor and breathtaking descriptions of the American wildlife that proves to be the key to Gus’ spiritual awakening.<br /><br />Highly reminiscent of <i>Mary Oliver</i>’s naturalistic poetry and <i>R.W. Emerson</i>’s philosophical pantheism, Duncan’s prose pulsates with boundless love for the natural world. Man’s soul is irretrievably connected to it and the divine is to be found everywhere in the here and the now; not in liturgical or organized prayer. Man’s soul should be the result of intimate, personal introspection rather the dogmatic notion of sacred purity.<br />Duncan is as intense as he is unpretentious and his words flow naturally and unfeigned like the waters of a silvery river that seeks its final release into the vast ocean. <br /><br />Attitude changes everything. If he is respectful, the fisherman might become one with the hooked salmon, and the light line that unites them is the answer to the silent questioning of <i>The River Why</i>. <br />What I discovered at the end of Gus’ line has become my favorite place to be: the self melted with the Eternal Now, the senses becoming what they perceive, the invisible line of light and love, so fine, so fragile and yet so indestructible that binds us, everything, all of it, together, secured in tight-knit gratefulness.<br />Gus finally understood that the river wasn’t asking why, it was sculpting the answer with its unstoppable course, from its source to the sea.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16012585115319577279" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer12081246566197863719">David James Duncan is a hero in our home, an integral part of my oldest son's coming of age. In 1991 Aaron was going to turn 16, and I had just finished reading The River Why for the first time. For dozens of reasons I fell in love with the book, and wanted to share the book with Aaron, and avid reader himself. <br/><br/>The paperback copy I had was a later edition, and I sent my copy to the Sierra Club in San Francisco, explaining that I knew they would not share the author's address, could they please s</span>
  <span id="freeText12081246566197863719" style="display:none">David James Duncan is a hero in our home, an integral part of my oldest son's coming of age. In 1991 Aaron was going to turn 16, and I had just finished reading The River Why for the first time. For dozens of reasons I fell in love with the book, and wanted to share the book with Aaron, and avid reader himself. <br /><br />The paperback copy I had was a later edition, and I sent my copy to the Sierra Club in San Francisco, explaining that I knew they would not share the author's address, could they please send on my edition, with my note requesting the author sign my copy, to be given to my son. <br /><br />A month or so passed, and one day I received a package in the mail, from David James Duncan, who had replaced my paperback with a first edition (in a note to me he wrote he "hoped I did not mind" replacing the paperback! The inscription reads "To Aaron, on the occasion of your 16th birthday, hoping your own path on the river why is full of twist's and turns that are wonderful." When he wrote the word "why" it was as a meandering river....<br /><br />Of course this book, and the letters that came with it, are one of my son's treasures, and now, as he prepares to have his first child, a boy, he plans on sharing this gift with him one day. </span>
  <a data-text-id="12081246566197863719" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer7572184201917525701">Well, this is now my favorite book, bar none. In fact, I liked this book so much I feel half inclined to go back and deduct a star from all of my other 'read' books just so this 5 star one can stand out. <br/><br/>It had aspects of all of my favorite books combined. <br/>Comedy and fantastic writing that is at times beautifully simple, and intellectually dense. <br/>Every character stood out as an incredibly interesting individual, so much so that if the author hadn't of said this was a work of fiction himself I wo</span>
  <span id="freeText7572184201917525701" style="display:none">Well, this is now my favorite book, bar none. In fact, I liked this book so much I feel half inclined to go back and deduct a star from all of my other 'read' books just so this 5 star one can stand out. <br /><br />It had aspects of all of my favorite books combined. <br />Comedy and fantastic writing that is at times beautifully simple, and intellectually dense. <br />Every character stood out as an incredibly interesting individual, so much so that if the author hadn't of said this was a work of fiction himself I wouldn't have believed it. <br />Deep philosophy blends beautifully next to all the other humdrums of life. And then, three quarters of the way through the book I'm surprised by a love story! One that didn't even need the whole book for me to get attached to it. <br /><br />My god... this book was genius.<br />If you think you need to like fishing to appreciate this book, you're wrong. The only thing you really need is an appreciation for nature. </span>

<div id="review_4663943" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/4663943" />
    <a title="Cheri" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/106692-cheri"><img alt="Cheri" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601402708p2/106692.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/4663943?book_show_action=true">Feb 08, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cheri" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cheri" href="/user/show/106692-cheri">Cheri</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/25932906-the-river-why">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/106692-cheri?shelf=spirituality">spirituality</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/106692-cheri?shelf=2019">2019</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/106692-cheri?shelf=family-relationships">family-relationships</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/106692-cheri?shelf=father-son-relationships">father-son-relationships</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/106692-cheri?shelf=nature">nature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/106692-cheri?shelf=oregon">oregon</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/106692-cheri?shelf=fishing">fishing</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/106692-cheri?shelf=fishing-industry">fishing-industry</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer4663943" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12019326403394825117"><i>”Many men go fishing all of their lives without knowing it is not fish they are after.”</i><br/>				--- Henry David Thoreau <br/><br/>Part spiritual<i>ishy</i>-quest, part fishing tales, part family drama, add in a quirky, fanciful introduction to romance, a coming-of-age tale, some interestingly eccentric characters with their own strange stories to share, and a reverent approach to Nature – all set in / on / near the mythical Tamawanis River in Oregon. <br/><br/>I added this book to my to-read list three years ago, right after I</span>
  <span id="freeText12019326403394825117" style="display:none"><i>”Many men go fishing all of their lives without knowing it is not fish they are after.”</i><br />				--- Henry David Thoreau <br /><br />Part spiritual<i>ishy</i>-quest, part fishing tales, part family drama, add in a quirky, fanciful introduction to romance, a coming-of-age tale, some interestingly eccentric characters with their own strange stories to share, and a reverent approach to Nature – all set in / on / near the mythical Tamawanis River in Oregon. <br /><br />I added this book to my to-read list three years ago, right after I finished reading David James Duncan’s <i>‘The Brothers K,”</i> which, initially, I thought I might end up loving perhaps a bit more than this one as a story, but this story has its own charms, and it will be a long time before I forget this one with its memorable characters and the setting. <br /><br /><i>There were the letters, there was the word: plain as water, in a flowing, utterly uncrabbed hand, current, erosion, gravity, and chance had written <b>why </b>upon the valley floor! Billions of ever-changing, ever-the-same gallons of gurgling sun-and-moon-washed ink, spelling forever, in plain English, <b>why</b>. It was incredible. It had to be kidding. Rivers can’t write, let alone ask questions. <br /><b>why </b>it said. It had a point. What did I know about what rivers could or couldn’t do? But granting it literacy, what did it mean by <b>why</b>? And was it asking me? I didn’t know. I looked upstream and down for a clue, but all I saw were the random scribbled curves of runs and rapids. Yet right below, in quarter-mile letters it had taken centuries to form, water—my favorite element—asked in the only language I could read, <b>why.</b></i><br /><br />While there is a “spiritual” / religious aspect to this story, don’t let that dissuade you since it is more of a loosely woven philosophy involving Nature interwoven with a sense of religious reverence for the world around us, many of which are relayed through allegorical fishing tales, some through introductions of other characters. <br /><br />Family is another strong theme in this story, from Gus’ parents, his father a gentleman’s fly fisherman, his mother strictly a <i>“raucous cowgirl”</i> who uses bait, to his younger brother Bill Bob who seems to float through his life as a connecting, if quiet, force that binds them together through a love of nature, and an acknowledgement of the grace of living, despite their differences. <br /><br /><b>"And so I learned what solitude really was. It was raw material – awesome, malleable, older than men or worlds or water. And it was <i>merciless</i> – for it let a man become precisely what he alone made of himself."</b></span>
  <a data-text-id="12019326403394825117" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_3106554538" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3106554538" />
    <a title="Joy D" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/50297885-joy-d"><img alt="Joy D" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1450639623p2/50297885.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3106554538?book_show_action=true">Sep 10, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Joy D" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Joy D" href="/user/show/50297885-joy-d">Joy D</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=literary-fiction">literary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=spirituality">spirituality</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=storytelling">storytelling</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=nature1">nature1</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=environmental">environmental</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=sports">sports</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=humor">humor</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/50297885-joy-d?shelf=zck">zck</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3106554538" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1511284692481207358">At age twenty, Gus Orviston tells of his life growing up in rural Oregon in a fishing family. His dad writes about fishing. His parents met while fishing. They constantly debate the merits of bait versus fly fishing. Upon graduating from high school, he believes his life will be complete if he can achieve the “ultimate schedule” of doing nothing but eating, sleeping, and fishing. So, he moves away from home to an isolated cabin near a stream. Gus starts to notice the impact of human activity on </span>
  <span id="freeText1511284692481207358" style="display:none">At age twenty, Gus Orviston tells of his life growing up in rural Oregon in a fishing family. His dad writes about fishing. His parents met while fishing. They constantly debate the merits of bait versus fly fishing. Upon graduating from high school, he believes his life will be complete if he can achieve the “ultimate schedule” of doing nothing but eating, sleeping, and fishing. So, he moves away from home to an isolated cabin near a stream. Gus starts to notice the impact of human activity on his surroundings, which changes his outlook. He eventually figures out that there needs to be more to his life than a single-minded pursuit, and he branches out. <br /><br />This novel is so much more than a book about fishing. It is about finding one’s place in the world. It contains musings about love, spirituality, and life. Fishing becomes a metaphor for a search for meaning in life. It expresses a reverence for the earth and its creatures. <br /><br />The author weaves together beautiful descriptions of nature, a number of mini-stories, and a great deal of humor. There are a number of eccentric characters – a five-year-old child nicknamed Hemingway, a dog named Descartes, and a young woman who fishes from a tree. I found it extremely creative, and though I am far from a fisherman, I very much enjoyed this uplifting story. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1511284692481207358" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_698999967" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/698999967" />
    <a title="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4728718-algernon-darth-anyan"><img alt="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1553101944p2/4728718.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/698999967?book_show_action=true">Mar 25, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Algernon (Darth Anyan)" href="/user/show/4728718-algernon-darth-anyan">Algernon (Darth Anyan)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4728718-algernon-darth-anyan?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4728718-algernon-darth-anyan?shelf=2020">2020</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer698999967" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17582837029676594019"><b> O the gallant fisher’s life,<br/>It is the best of any!<br/>‘Tis full of pleasure, void of strife<br/>And ’tis beloved by many. </b><br/><br/>Sometimes it’s good to go outside your comfort zone and try something completely different. I’ve never been interested in fishing, despite some close friends’ repeated invitations to come along on their trips. Turns out I’ve been a fisherman all my life and didn’t know it. Gus, the young man who is crazy about fishing in this seminal novel, is the one who opened my eyes to the deeper</span>
  <span id="freeText17582837029676594019" style="display:none"><b> O the gallant fisher’s life,<br />It is the best of any!<br />‘Tis full of pleasure, void of strife<br />And ’tis beloved by many. </b><br /><br />Sometimes it’s good to go outside your comfort zone and try something completely different. I’ve never been interested in fishing, despite some close friends’ repeated invitations to come along on their trips. Turns out I’ve been a fisherman all my life and didn’t know it. Gus, the young man who is crazy about fishing in this seminal novel, is the one who opened my eyes to the deeper significance of the art. Born on the North Western coast of the US, Gus is the scion of a fishing family where the book of Izaak Walton, provider of my opening quote, is both a source of inspiration and of fiery controversy between his fly-fishing father and his bait-fishing mother.<br /><br /><b> Years before I could have put it into words, I realized that my fate would lead me beside still waters, beside rough waters, beside blue, green, muddy, clear, and salt waters. From the beginning my mind and heart were so taken up with the liquid element that nearly every other thing on the earth’s bulbous face struck me as irrelevant, distracting, a waste of my time. </b><br /><br />The first part of the story is the bait at the end of the line with which the reader is lured to take a bite and sample the goods. And I for one was captivated: hook, line and sinker! I’ve rarely encountered such exuberance, such joy in the use of language as displayed by the account of young Gus coming into this world and of his growing up in this fantastically competitive and argumentative household. <i> Zounds, Drat and Bother the Bleeding Fates! </i> exclaims his upper class English father, nicknamed H2O by his scion and narrator, every time he is challenged in his purist approach to the art of fly fishing. In the opposite corner sits his mother, a local Portland redneck with an irreverent attitude and a knack for out-fishing her famous husband:<br /><br /><i> “Glum AGIN? Cheer up, boy! Always limpin’ around with a burr in yer ass! Smile, dammit!” </i><br /><br />Later in the novel, my admiration for the humour and the language was matched by my interest in the elegance and beauty of the allegory between fishing and spiritual emancipation. What could have been a typical coming of age story of a gloomy teenager leaving the family nest and striking out on his own in the wide world, becomes under the pen of David James Duncan a major philosophical treaty about the condition of the modern man in his search for meaning. The theme is spelled out in fishing analogies all through the text, but probably the most clear resume comes in the afterword to the anniversary edition that I’ve read:<br /><br /><b> A migration I see humanity dying to make is not geographical: it’s a journey from lives governed by the head to lives governed by the heart. </b><br /><br />Gus starts his journey by renouncing a college education in order to rent a solitary cabin in a wild part of Oregon, by the side of a salmon rich river, where he hopes to prove to his father and to his mother that he is the very best fisherman of all. By catching more fish than everybody else, Gus hopes to prove his worth in the eyes of his peers. He has a lot of native talent from his mother, a good training from his father, talent, determination, method: everything he needs to become the perfect scientific angler, the epitome of the Western ethos of Nature being there to be conquered, subdued by the daring pioneer spirit.<br /><br /><b> If the fool had only known how to swim there wouldn’t be any of this seething in my brain – this What-is-Death / What-is-Life / Why-am-I-here / What-am-I-for stuff. What use were such questions? Hobgoblins – that’s all they were – noisy abstract swill good for nothing but scaring and depressing the hell out of everybody they occurred to ... </b><br /><br />The breaking point of this blind rush into the Guinness Book of Records for Fishing comes when the river in question, just like the young Gus mind, becomes engulfed in a deep fog. Out of this unknown space comes floating by the dead body of a fellow fisherman, prompting the arrival of the Big Questions, those hobgoblins so beloved by the Russian classic novelists. The river is asking Gus already :WHY?<br /><br /><b> But what was the difference between need and greed? How many fish could a man kill without his killing becoming wanton? </b><br /><br />Which brings me to the third and final reason I am sure this novel will become one of my all time favorites: It doesn’t simply expose the problems faced by modern man, it honestly tries to find a better way forward, both for the individual redemption and for the fate of a finely balanced natural world that has experienced enough pillaging, pollution and waste.<br /><br />The smartest move Gus makes once those hobgoblin questions start to gnaw at his peace of mind is to go out of his shell of self-interest and manic-obsessive career planning and start connecting with the people who live nearby – making friends, giving free of his talent and of his time, offering friendship and asking for help. The change is a bit abrupt, narrative-wise, but this novel is after all an allegory, not a true biography. What’s more, Gus is a fisherman, and we all know that the second best thing these people love is to tell stories, the more incredible and far-fetched, the better. Happy / curious Gus is a lot more enchanting than gloomy / fishing obsessed Gus. The portraits of the oddball neighbors and the funny sketches that introduce them mark a return to the more effervescent pages from the debut of the novel. <br /><br />What’s changed is this deep undercurrent of spiritual thirst and social engagement that is driving Gus to further explore the limits of his understanding of the world. Some of the pages are still filled with funny anecdotes about fishing and living like a savage in the middle of nowhere, but there’s a lot of pain when Gus looks up at the clear-cut mountain tops, at the rivers blocked for fish by concrete dams or suffocated by suburban or industrial waste. The change of heart in young Gus is clearly reflected in his new rants about <i> the smug ingratitude, the attitude that assumed the world and its creatures owed us everything we could catch, shoot, tear out, alter, plunder, devour ... and we owed the world nothing in return. </i><br /><br />One of the key new characters introduced in this middle part of the novel is Titus, a wannabee city fisherman with an extraordinary knack for literary references. The meeting between Gus and Titus goes something like this:<br /><br /><b> “So if you’re willing to risk your life initiating me into the mysteries of fishing, the least I could do in return is introduce you to the forgotten science of philosophy. What do you say?”<br />I shrugged. “What have I got to lose?”<br />“Your unhappiness,” he said. </b><br /><br />Human interaction can take you a long way on the path to a healthy mind and spirit, but we as modern men are also blessed with easy access to the wisdom of past ages. We have only ourselves to blame if we close our minds to the writings of these great thinkers, preferring instead to fill them with cheap escapist thrills, competitive sports, political propaganda or reality shows. Gus, under the tutelage of his new friend, is drinking deeply from this fountain of wisdom, reminded that the word philosophy translates as a love for thinking.<br /><br /><b> I soon found myself eyeing the covers of unknown books with the same sense of expectancy I felt when scrutinizing the waters of a new stream. </b><br /><br />Almost as strong an influence as Titus is Bill Bob, the geeky little brother of our hero Gus, the only one in the family completely immune to the fishing virus. If Titus is a symbol of the life of the intellect, Bill Bob is cast as the avatar of common sense and innocence. The little boy reiterates the need for listening to the heart, for a faith in a higher purpose than the immediate needs of living day to day. Bill Bob, with his multiple and divergent hobbies, is also the best illustration of a passion for all thing living or inanimate, a burning curiosity that knows no bounds.<br /><br />Closing the middle section of the novel is the third encounter that would shape the future life of fisherman Gus – the most elusive and the most alluring bait in every young man’s imagination:<br /><br /><b> I wasn’t used to looking at such things, let alone in trees, let alone fishing, let alone slender and golden-skinned and young and blond and solitary and, um – the pole. </b><br /><br />Love comes knocking at his door, but unfortunately Gus spent most of his youth blinded by his obsession with fishing instead of honing his social skills, so this slippery eel of a girl named Eddy escapes his clutches.<br /><br />The third and final part of the book returns to an introspective mood. Gus, now sustained by his new friends, nevertheless turns his eyes inwards and makes a solitary journey, similar in many ways to the myth of the Hero of a Thousand Faces described by Joseph Campbell or Mircea Eliade, towards the source of all wisdom, the hidden spring of his river WHY?<br /><br /><i> They were not much like the usual sacred signs – but fishing was hardly an orthodox faith ... And these things had been given as gifts – like rain, like rivers – unlooked for, unasked for: I had to follow the signs that I was given, as rivers follow valleys, as spring follows winter, as leaves turn and salmon spawn and geese fly south in October. I couldn’t trade the trail these images blazed for me for a straight and narrow way – not when water’s ways, meandering and free flowing, had always been my love. </i><br /><br />Organized religion and blind faith are some of the other things that smell fishy to me and often prompt me to give such material a wide detour, but I do believe in the need for self-examination, in the need for a higher purpose in life and in finding your own individual way to salvation. David James Duncan, through the voice of Gus, surprised me a little by turning the novel into a late argument for the existence of a Biblical sort of God, but the spiritual search among the mountain tops, deep glades, clear rivers and ocean shores, in the fall of a gentle rain or in the jump of a fish out of the waves, is a clear echo of my own journey from big city to forest or mountain trail whenever I need to recharge my batteries. What I lack in articulating my passion for nature and life is the eloquence of a Gus or Duncan, who went through the valley of shadow and brought back not a message of despair but one of beauty and hope.<br /><br /><b> I awoke before dawn. The morning star was twinkling through the same opening in the cedars and the world was too wide and lovely to leave unexplored. </b><br /><br />in another place: <i> What I realized was that a mecca isn’t worth much if it’s not a place inside you more than a place in the world. </i> <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[ the source is everywhere  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />I am a little envious of Gus going though his moment of revelation at such a young age and coming back down from the mountain to enjoy the fruits of his spiritual quest. But I think he deserves a good break from the author, a final message that there might be a lot of pain in the world today, but there are still beautiful people around, and beautiful rivers where the salmon are still making their yearly pilgrimage against the tides of fate in order to lay the foundation for a new generation. It’s a very fragile ecosystem that needs our care and our love.<br /><br /><b> ... yet while the rain fell I didn’t fish – only watched and rested, and I was lulled and cradled, caressed, and enveloped in a cool, mothering touch that washed away the wounds of summer; and my old, unmitigated longings – even the longings for fish, for Eddy, for the Friend – were changed from gnawing, aching dissatisfactions into a kind of sad, silent music, and the hollow place those longings had carved in me became a kind of sanctuary, an emptiness I grew used to, grew satisfied to leave unfilled. </b><br /><br />&gt;&gt;&gt;&lt;&lt;&lt;&gt;&gt;&gt;&lt;&lt;&lt;<br /><br />Bohumil Hrabal wrote somewhere that <i> “If a book has anything to say, it burns with a quiet laugh, because any book worth its salt points up and out of itself.” </i><br />David James Duncan has wisely provided for us the clues for continuing the search by including in each chapter heading an epigram or two from his favorites writers, poets and sources of inspiration. Beside the often quoted Izaak Walton, I’ve bookmarked several references that tempt me strongly. A poem by W B Yeats: “The Song of Wandering Aengus” or a quote by W H Auden : <i> ‘A culture is no better than its woods.’ </i> or new authors to look up like Alice Likowski or Jim Harrison.<br />Of course, the other novel written by David James Duncan has also become a priority for me.</span>
  <a data-text-id="17582837029676594019" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer10177551351221498516">Some books you just read.<br/>Some books draw you in.<br/>Some books read you-- and in the process lay you out, naked and utterly absorbed in every sensation and feeling as though you were just born.<br/><br/>Welcome to The River Why.<br/><br/>I never thought of fishing and philosophy as a duo.   <span id="freeText10177551351221498516" style="display:none">Some books you just read.<br />Some books draw you in.<br />Some books read you-- and in the process lay you out, naked and utterly absorbed in every sensation and feeling as though you were just born.<br /><br />Welcome to The River Why.<br /><br />I never thought of fishing and philosophy as a duo. I don't even particularly care that much about fishing (despite having done so with my grandfather when I was a little girl).<br />But Duncan has created a story so rich in thought and depth, that even the technicalities of fly making, casting, rod building, and water reading are irresistible.<br />And with characters so real, they jumped off the page and sat in the room with me while I read.<br />And humor that had me laughing aloud so often that those around me probably questioned my sanity.<br /><br />It has been a long time since I was actually hesitant to pick up a book and begin reading again-- because it never failed to pull me in ... yet I never wanted the book to end.<br />Rich, beautiful, poetic, smart, funny.<br /><br />I will need a few days to process and bask before cracking open the cover on anything else now.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer23042655353581127">This novel tells the story of young fishing prodigy Gus Orviston and his madcap, fishing-obsessed family. After graduating from high school, Gus leaves home so he can be free of distractions and devote himself entirely to fishing. In the process and despite himself, Gus comes to discover the joys of community, romantic love, and eventually, God.<br/><br/>It’s hard for me to express just how much I love this book.   <span id="freeText23042655353581127" style="display:none">This novel tells the story of young fishing prodigy Gus Orviston and his madcap, fishing-obsessed family. After graduating from high school, Gus leaves home so he can be free of distractions and devote himself entirely to fishing. In the process and despite himself, Gus comes to discover the joys of community, romantic love, and eventually, God.<br /><br />It’s hard for me to express just how much I love this book. One of the biggest reasons why is because it’s laugh-out-loud hysterical. There are just not very many books dealing with spiritual matters that are so fantastically funny. But this one probably makes up for the lack of many others. Go. Read it now. You’ll thank me, I swear.<br /><br /></span>

  <span id="freeText11015131482688559112" style="display:none">I am two parts surprised to one part in love with this book. I've never met a person like him yet major aspects of his character run through some of my favorite people. He matures into a young man that I would like to sit next to on an 8 hour bus ride. <div id="review_33826802" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/33826802" />
    <a title="Deb" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1564887-deb"><img alt="Deb" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1499187533p2/1564887.jpg" /></a>

<div id="review_493330813" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/493330813" />
    <a title="James R" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3972936-james-r"><img alt="James R" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1332015163p2/3972936.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/493330813?book_show_action=true">Jan 02, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="James R" class="user" itemprop="url" name="James R" href="/user/show/3972936-james-r">James R</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer493330813" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12809312686539955548">I tried really hard to love this book, but couldn't. I kept hoping it would grab me. Generally I find books whose authors respect and revere the natural world, who write knowledgeably and often beautifully about it, and whose characters struggle with existential questions to be immensely satisfying and engaging, so I think I understand why others count this as a 5 star favorite. My reaction was much the same as the editors who rejected it and who Duncan described in the Afterward he wrote for th</span>
  <span id="freeText12809312686539955548" style="display:none">I tried really hard to love this book, but couldn't. I kept hoping it would grab me. Generally I find books whose authors respect and revere the natural world, who write knowledgeably and often beautifully about it, and whose characters struggle with existential questions to be immensely satisfying and engaging, so I think I understand why others count this as a 5 star favorite. My reaction was much the same as the editors who rejected it and who Duncan described in the Afterward he wrote for the 20th anniversary edition. It was too long with too much exposition. Maybe if I were a fisher the, for me, tediously long descriptions of fishing for this or that kind of fish with this or that kind of equipment and technique would have captured me. But they didn't they just muddied the waters so to speak. I don't want to discourage anyone from reading The River Why. I certainly am glad I did. I just wish I had enjoyed it more.</span>

<div id="review_562938253" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/562938253" />
    <a title="JJVid" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/11143358-jjvid"><img alt="JJVid" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1342920697p2/11143358.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/562938253?book_show_action=true">Mar 16, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="JJVid" class="user" itemprop="url" name="JJVid" href="/user/show/11143358-jjvid">JJVid</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11143358-jjvid?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11143358-jjvid?shelf=religion">religion</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/11143358-jjvid?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer562938253" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14787460425826350349">God (or religion, spirituality, the One, etc) can be found anywhere and <i>should</i> be a product of your own idiosyncratic life experiences. Gus, the narrator/fisher hero of <i>The River Why</i>, finds his God among the river. The line of light. Prying himself away from toxic relationship with his family, Gus endeavors an "ideal" life along the river Why somewhere near Oregon. Through isolation from others and a total fixation on his singular passion, fishing, Gus pursues his notion of the perfect life, but</span>
  <span id="freeText14787460425826350349" style="display:none">God (or religion, spirituality, the One, etc) can be found anywhere and <i>should</i> be a product of your own idiosyncratic life experiences. Gus, the narrator/fisher hero of <i>The River Why</i>, finds his God among the river. The line of light. Prying himself away from toxic relationship with his family, Gus endeavors an "ideal" life along the river Why somewhere near Oregon. Through isolation from others and a total fixation on his singular passion, fishing, Gus pursues his notion of the perfect life, but he is quickly disillusioned. A life solo, and circumstances forced upon Gus unwittingly, cause the narrator/fisher hero to reevaluate what it means to live a good life, and forces him to reinterpret his life and relations prior to the river Why. <br /><br />I've heard this called a "coming of age story", but doing so pigeonholes this novel and colors the expectations of would-be readers. This is much, much more than a mere recapitulation of a boyhood maturing. This book tells the story of a boy who begins to truly understand himself, and the reader inevitably reflects upon the same thoughts and understand hirself better too. <br /><br />Normally I am capable of reviewing a novel in detail and condensing a summary easily, but the scope and power of this novel are too profound to offhandedly comment upon. I'll update this review over the weekend, since I cannot do it justice at present, but I'd like to inform any (hypothetical) reader in the present that this was the best and most significant work I've read in a very long time. Not a single page was dull, dozens of pages were read without even noticing the page numbers, and I had to force myself to ration my reading so that I could wake up for work in the morning in a pseudo-conscious state. I'm simultaneously upset that I cannot write much more on this novel than present, and dumbfounded at how much I want to convey.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14787460425826350349" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/245730988" />
    <a title="Becky" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1558310-becky"><img alt="Becky" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1444251999p2/1558310.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/245730988?book_show_action=true">Dec 11, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Becky" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Becky" href="/user/show/1558310-becky">Becky</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer245730988" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3581860628569660060">I have really mixed feelings about this book. I keep going back and forth between giving it 3 stars and giving it 4 (yes, I know, it's super important and I should anguish over getting it right). I guess I've settled on 4 for now. I loved a lot of this book. But, there was something strange about it, like, some things didn't seem to "fit" with the rest of the <i>feel</i> of the book. For example, the majority of the characters other than Gus were very exaggerated and cartoonish. So much so that the las</span>
  <span id="freeText3581860628569660060" style="display:none">I have really mixed feelings about this book. I keep going back and forth between giving it 3 stars and giving it 4 (yes, I know, it's super important and I should anguish over getting it right). I guess I've settled on 4 for now. I loved a lot of this book. But, there was something strange about it, like, some things didn't seem to "fit" with the rest of the <i>feel</i> of the book. For example, the majority of the characters other than Gus were very exaggerated and cartoonish. So much so that the last chapter (which I think felt like an afterthought and didn't need to be there), when he talks about Vietnam, it caught me off-guard because the majority of the characters were so silly that I forgot this all was supposed to be taking place in the real world in an actual time period.<br /><br />I liked Gus a lot and really loved his existential musings throughout the book. I like David James Duncan's writing, for the most part (I loved the Brothers K and highly recommend it), but parts of this book became tedious to read for me. At times I wanted to scream "OMG I GET THE POINT!"<br /><br />still worth a read, though, my opinion. my copy refers to itself as the "unabridged" which leads me to believe there is an abridged version which sounds perfect.<br /><br />last note: if you're only going to read one book about philosophy and fishing, read A River Runs Through It. </span>
  <a data-text-id="3581860628569660060" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText5581813770766776170" style="display:none">I have never been that interested in fishing, and this book makes me want to fish! It's so clever...funny, (the opening paragraph had me laughing) and enlightening. Gus, the main character grows up fishing, in this crazy, fishing-obsessed, little family, and then he strikes out on his own, comes to know himself and what's really, most important to him. <br /><br /><br />Towards the end of the book, I read this passage, and then re-read it and re-read at least ten times...<br /><br /> "Dawn came up behind the hills, extending her old fingertips of rose. I plodded on toward the outstretched fingers and the glimmering continued; fish-bites, birth pangs, I didn't know what they were. But the further I walked, the less I cared. It was enough to feel them.<br />I trudged on, helpless to catch hold of things, but hopeful. And when the first sunlight lit upon the tallest ridge's highest vineleaf maple, when the rosy fingers faded into blue behind the mountain, when the vineleaf's leaves shone out in scarred and blazing scarlet atop that wave-like ridge of dull alder gold a chill shot from my thighs to the top of my head, surged up on my backbone, again and again--for in that moment I felt as though an oldest, greatest, longest-lost Friend had come to walk the road, unseen beside me..."<br /><br /></span>

<div id="review_659968855" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/659968855" />
    <a title="Kevin Neilson" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2088163-kevin-neilson"><img alt="Kevin Neilson" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1242063463p2/2088163.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/659968855?book_show_action=true">Jul 05, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kevin Neilson" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kevin Neilson" href="/user/show/2088163-kevin-neilson">Kevin Neilson</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer659968855" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10062737641720485036">There is a good book hidden inside <i>The River Why</i>, though I couldn't call it a good book. It's a bit of a mess, all over the map. In the afterword the author says some publishers wanted to whittle it down. Sometimes the publishers are right! Some parts are really funny, namely the sections about his parents and upbringing. I really liked the romance, even though it seemed like the unrealistic fantasy of a fly-fishing teenager (or immature adult like yours truly). I can assure you that few fly-fis</span>
  <span id="freeText10062737641720485036" style="display:none">There is a good book hidden inside <i>The River Why</i>, though I couldn't call it a good book. It's a bit of a mess, all over the map. In the afterword the author says some publishers wanted to whittle it down. Sometimes the publishers are right! Some parts are really funny, namely the sections about his parents and upbringing. I really liked the romance, even though it seemed like the unrealistic fantasy of a fly-fishing teenager (or immature adult like yours truly). I can assure you that few fly-fishermen are women, and among those there are probably not a large number who are twenty, look like a centerfold, and angle in the buff. That is why we have fiction, I suppose: to dream.<br /><br />The characters of Titus and Bill-Bob should probably have been eliminated entirely. Dialogue between Gus and Titus is risible. Many of the long asides about philosophy grow rapidly tiresome. "A River Runs Through It" is philosophical, but not tiresome. I'm pretty sure it is the only decent novel about fly-fishing, but at least there is one. (And some short stories and chapters within Hemingway's oeuvre.)<br /><br />Do not watch the movie. It is an even bigger mess than the novel. If Eddy's angling scenes had been a little less PG-rated, it might have redeemed itself the slightest bit.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10062737641720485036" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer10320270309977239761">I know beans about fly-fishing, and as a kid I grew to hate fishing because of early morning forays with my father and one of his brothers wherein I worked at cutting bait, cleaning fish, and other cold, unpleasant tasks. This is a great novel even though it looks like it's about fishing. It's a love story, a story of the struggle with God, and a conservationist story. Read it, read it, read it!</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer15121993365717956954">"And so I learned what solitude really was. It was raw material- awesome, malleable, older than men or worlds or water. And it was <i>merciless</i> -for it let a man become precisely what he alone made of himself."<br/><br/>First let me say that I am neither religious nor "spiritual". I find books about discovering one's spirituality tiresome. I am solidly in the secular materialist camp. So with that said, let me now say that I loved this book. I loved it despite the moral of the tale, which is that God is (qui</span>
  <span id="freeText15121993365717956954" style="display:none">"And so I learned what solitude really was. It was raw material- awesome, malleable, older than men or worlds or water. And it was <i>merciless</i> -for it let a man become precisely what he alone made of himself."<br /><br />First let me say that I am neither religious nor "spiritual". I find books about discovering one's spirituality tiresome. I am solidly in the secular materialist camp. So with that said, let me now say that I loved this book. I loved it despite the moral of the tale, which is that God is (quite literally) love. I loved Gus Orviston's journey from glum member of a dysfunctional fishing family, to his time spent as an misanthropic hermit, to his awakening to the natural world and to the people around him. I loved his evolving views on fishing. The last chapter, cleverly called "The Last Chapter", seemed tacked on and unnecessary, but otherwise, a thoroughly enjoyable read. </span>

<div id="review_230199059" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/230199059" />
    <a title="Bryce" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3159559-bryce"><img alt="Bryce" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1515113930p2/3159559.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/230199059?book_show_action=true">Nov 02, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bryce" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bryce" href="/user/show/3159559-bryce">Bryce</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer230199059" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8846165521715831221">I first read this novel in April 2004. I chose to re-read it during the past week while I was on a military mission outside of the continental US. During a long flight and in the evenings following very long tiring days of stressful work, I re-read one of my all-time favorite works of fiction. This novel is not just about fishing (although it helps to be a fisherman and understand the allure of the pastime) but about finding balance in life, finding one's heart and a connection to a Higher Power</span>
  <span id="freeText8846165521715831221" style="display:none">I first read this novel in April 2004. I chose to re-read it during the past week while I was on a military mission outside of the continental US. During a long flight and in the evenings following very long tiring days of stressful work, I re-read one of my all-time favorite works of fiction. This novel is not just about fishing (although it helps to be a fisherman and understand the allure of the pastime) but about finding balance in life, finding one's heart and a connection to a Higher Power, of human relationships, and about stewardship (of the earth and humanity). The book inspires me to find joy in little things, in service, in love, in standing for what is true and precious and lasting. I recommend this book to anyone, even if you don't like fishing. If you have never fished and want to try it, I am always available.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8846165521715831221" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_3392090762" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3392090762" />
    <a title="Owen Toepfer" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/116909140-owen-toepfer"><img alt="Owen Toepfer" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1592666116p2/116909140.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3392090762?book_show_action=true">Jun 20, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Owen Toepfer" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Owen Toepfer" href="/user/show/116909140-owen-toepfer">Owen Toepfer</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3392090762" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8736777131990284443">David James Duncan’s prose is at times miraculous. Not only is his writing *aesthetically* masterful, but each sentence is packed with real *substance*. He knows he is virtuosic, but he also knows how not to overdo it (unlike many authors in his class). Think David Foster Wallace if David Foster Wallace had exhibited more authorial restraint. After a while, one starts to pick up on his favorite literary tricks—but they retain much of their luster nevertheless.<br/><br/>Essentially a story about a confused</span>
  <span id="freeText8736777131990284443" style="display:none">David James Duncan’s prose is at times miraculous. Not only is his writing *aesthetically* masterful, but each sentence is packed with real *substance*. He knows he is virtuosic, but he also knows how not to overdo it (unlike many authors in his class). Think David Foster Wallace if David Foster Wallace had exhibited more authorial restraint. After a while, one starts to pick up on his favorite literary tricks—but they retain much of their luster nevertheless.<br /><br />Essentially a story about a confused and ascetic young man’s search for meaning, Duncan is free to philosophize and explore the “why”s of life (hence the title). I think one of the best features of the book is the way Duncan is able to demonstrate the importance of community and taking responsibility for our neighbors—and how self-sacrifice and -gift in many ways are the answer to those “why”s. And despite Duncan’s clear (and at times extremely annoying and contrived) tendency toward Universalism and vague Deism, there are a few passages in the book that capture Christian spirituality beautifully.<br /><br />A bonus: you learn a lot about fish and the art of fly fishing, and just about every single character is hilarious or fascinating or both.<br /><br />It’s probably more of a 3.5-star book, but I rounded up.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8736777131990284443" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3166697288?book_show_action=true">Apr 23, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matthew McDavid" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matthew McDavid" href="/user/show/109408857-matthew-mcdavid">Matthew McDavid</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3166697288" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9092478648282371294">Absolutely incredible. One of the best books I’ve ever read. One of those books that makes me sad I’m finished and can’t experience it for the first time again.<br/><br/>The last quarter of this book is a spiritual journey and I have no idea what it was that I experienced. But I loved it. </span>

          </span>

<div id="review_118936281" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/118936281" />
    <a title="Goody" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/809160-goody"><img alt="Goody" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/118936281?book_show_action=true">Aug 27, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Goody" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Goody" href="/user/show/809160-goody">Goody</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Your inner nineteen-year-old</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Goody by: </span>
      My parents
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/809160-goody?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer118936281" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12857894124164515648">I started August 27—the day I bought my new favorite book for a dollar—with some anxious-making personal assistant work followed by enjoyable, effortless quality time with a pseudo-ex who also offered me casting referrals over lunch. My appointments fufilled, I walked uptown from Union Square and sat in Madison Square Park to write. It was one of those wonderful, optimistic days when the shade is just right, and somehow the smells of a park on Manhattan—chlorophyll, pigeons, exhaust, cooking con</span>
  <span id="freeText12857894124164515648" style="display:none">I started August 27—the day I bought my new favorite book for a dollar—with some anxious-making personal assistant work followed by enjoyable, effortless quality time with a pseudo-ex who also offered me casting referrals over lunch. My appointments fufilled, I walked uptown from Union Square and sat in Madison Square Park to write. It was one of those wonderful, optimistic days when the shade is just right, and somehow the smells of a park on Manhattan—chlorophyll, pigeons, exhaust, cooking concrete, dog poop—became perfectly therapeutic. I was filled with a sense of possibility. Everyone was beautiful. <br /><br />I called a friend, who’d wanted to meet me to watch a movie (I was, sorry to say, craving <i>Eat, Pray, Love</i>). Waiting, I wandered from Madision Square Park to Grand Central. My friend decided to go home, so I started walking west to my subway station on the other side of Manhattan. I took the Very Long way home. I was in an introspective, romantic, adventurer’s mood. <br /><br />On the way, I stopped in a huge discount book/DVD/Manga shop. I was looking under the Cs for Michael Chabon, because I thought to re-read <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16707.Wonder_Boys" title="Wonder Boys by Michael Chabon" rel="nofollow noopener">Wonder Boys</a>, but instead, a familiar spine caught my eye. <i>The River Why</i>, by David James Duncan. $1. Paperback, pristine condition. It was written in 1983, a year before I was born. My parents and my older sister had liked it, so I, longing for adulthood, picked it up in sixth grade. I don’t think I’ve thought of it since.<br /><br /><blockquote>Mike James, 10/19/97<br />8th Period<br />Dear Mrs. Adkins,<br />	I am in the middle of the book <i>The River Why</i> on page 57 of 294 pages. This funny fiction is written as a narrative, but the narrator is completely made-up by the author, David James Duncan. The main character is Gus Orviston (the narrator) who fishes excessively on River Why. I’m not sure yet why it’s called the River Why, but I was looking at the table of contents, and saw the word “Why” written like [a cursive, meandering, scrawled] “Why”. I derived from this that the river was shaped like the word “why”. <br />	This book is an excellent book so far, and I expect it to stay that way. It’s about the life of the narrator, Gus, his family, and a whole bunch of other situations and ideas. <br />	In one part of this book, Gus’s dad, whom was nicknamed H2O (he fished excessively, also), gave a book called <i>The Compleat Angler</i> to Gus’s mother. <i>The Compleat Angler</i>’s typing had all the S’s looking like F’s, and the form of complete was British, so his mother was thrown, and didn’t like the book anyhow. She nicknamed it a “Difcourfe ‘bout fifh an’ Fifhin,’” which I thought was funny. <br />	Gus keeps a notebook in which he writes down fishing records and other datum, which he refers to as “The God Notebook.” His great-grandmother always complains to his parents in this book about how people “shouldn’t go fishing on Sundays, because the guy down there’ll get you in a net.” He wrote all the things that she said down in his notebook, and misheard one thing that said Jesus netted people up to heaven with tongues.<br />	I think this book is very original and funny in its own clever way, and so far, is one of the better books I’ve read. <br />				Conspicuously,<br />					Mike James <br /></blockquote><br /><br />In my own sixth-grader way, page 57 is as far as I got. I gave up once I got wind of young Gus’s plans to run away to live in a cabin by river and spend his time flyfishing. I’d have nothing to do with flyfishing, nor with a character alone in a cabin. Where would the dialogue go? Who would he talk to?<br />To run into <i>The River Why</i> now felt right. I saw it as a blessing for this twenty-something.<br />Walking down into my subway, I leafed through this unworn, unbroken copy and found an inscription I’d overlooked.<br /><br /><blockquote>Benjy, <br />	For some reason this reflective novel about family, friendship, and of course fishing has always reminded me of you. I know I have only just begun my quest to discover my own “truth,” and I really want to thank you for your help along that path. I really think you would be a great father, but besides that, you <u>are</u> a great friend. Hope you enjoy this book—I’m sure you’ll find more than a little of yourself hidden within its pages.<br />Your Friend, <br />[unintelligible]</blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="12857894124164515648" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1478242480" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1478242480" />
    <a title="Kogiopsis" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1462364-kogiopsis"><img alt="Kogiopsis" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1494267218p2/1462364.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1478242480?book_show_action=true">Dec 24, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kogiopsis" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kogiopsis" href="/user/show/1462364-kogiopsis">Kogiopsis</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1462364-kogiopsis?shelf=reviewed">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1478242480" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16614552465626668366">I guess every now and then we all have to step out of our reading comfort zones. My stomping ground is modern sci fi/fantasy, so this meditative piece of realistic fiction from the 80s made for quite a change. Had it not been recommended to me by my parents, I wouldn't have picked it up but hey, sometimes they have a point.<br/><br/>It's a strange little book, to be sure, oddly written (but beautifully, in my opinion). It's not for everyone. Personally, as someone with a fondness for the natural world and</span>
  <span id="freeText16614552465626668366" style="display:none">I guess every now and then we all have to step out of our reading comfort zones. My stomping ground is modern sci fi/fantasy, so this meditative piece of realistic fiction from the 80s made for quite a change. Had it not been recommended to me by my parents, I wouldn't have picked it up but hey, sometimes they have a point.<br /><br />It's a strange little book, to be sure, oddly written (but beautifully, in my opinion). It's not for everyone. Personally, as someone with a fondness for the natural world and for Oregon's wilderness, I enjoyed a lot of the rambling naturalistic digressions. The narrator's humor also worked for me, though I doubt it will for everyone.<br /><br />I think the level on which this book resonated with me the most was its connection of spirituality and nature. As someone who isn't religious, but is familiar with that sweeping, overpowering sensation of awe that comes from standing on top of a mountain or deep in a forest, Gus's awakening was a description of something that I'd experienced but had never quite been able to put into words. The rest of the book was enjoyable, funny at times and weirdly poignant at others, but it was his hike to the source of the Tamawanis that struck me the most.<br /><br />Unfortunately, there's a romance. And since this is a book written in the 80s by a male author... you can imagine how that goes. It's more of a prolonged teenaged boy's wet dream than a real romantic subplot, and that becomes even more clear when compared with Ma and H2O Orviston's relationship at the beginning of the book. That had conflict, change, depth - Gus and Eddy together just feels flat, as if she's his reward for growing up some and learning to think about people other than himself.<br /><br />Overall - a worthwhile read, for me at least, but stylistically definitely something you have to be in the mood for. If you're feeling meditative and missing wild places, this might work for you too.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16614552465626668366" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
